iii
Declaration of Authorship
I, Peter BRUUN-RASMUSSEN, declare that this thesis titled, “Predictive Analysis of Latent
Infections in a Large Cohort of Blood Donors Using Machine Learning and Data Mining”
and the work presented in it are my own. I conﬁrm that:
• This work was done wholly or mainly while in candidature for a research degree at
this University.
• Where any part of this thesis has previously been submitted for a degree or any
other qualiﬁcation at this University or any other institution, this has been clearly
stated.
• Where I have consulted the published work of others, this is always clearly at-
tributed.
• Where I have quoted from the work of others, the source is always given. With the
exception of such quotations, this thesis is entirely my own work.
• I have acknowledged all main sources of help.
• Where the thesis is based on work done by myself jointly with others, I have made
clear exactly what was done by others and what I have contributed myself.
Signed:
Date: 03/02-2018

v
Technical University of Denmark
& University of Copenhagen
Abstract
Faculty of Health and Medical Sciences
Novo Nordisk Foundation for Protein Research
Master of Science
Predictive Analysis of Latent Infections in a Large Cohort of Blood Donors Using
Machine Learning and Data Mining
by Peter BRUUN-RASMUSSEN
Purpose: In this work I present a predictive analysis of Toxoplasma gondii-infections in a
cohort of blood donors (N=3973) obtained from the Danish Blood Donor study, including
IgG-antibody levels against T. gondii and 43 features.
Methods: Prediction models were obtained by training a logistic regression, random
forest, and neural network. Training was done on data-subsets holding all available fea-
tures, and subsets holding only features regularly obtained at the Danish blood banks.
Results: The highest predictive performance was achieved when including all features
(AUC: 0.859, MCC: 0.535), yet, a decent performance was obtained from the blood bank
speciﬁc features only (AUC: 0.768, MCC: 0.391). The classiﬁers performed almost equally
and the most important features were found to be Herpes simplex virus-1, Cytomegalovirus,
C-reactive protein, erythrocyte hemoglobin concentration, and variance in the size of ery-
throcytes.
Conclusion: The prediction models show potential of being used as a low-cost high-
throughput screening tool in a blood bank setting. Hereby, the potential risk of transmis-
sion of T. gondii from donor to recipient could be avoided. Furthermore, it is proposed to
use the models in association studies of the DBDS, where large unlabelled blood donor
data is available.
However, further tuning and testing of the models are needed before implemented.

Acknowledgements
I am particular appreciative for the guidance and support provided by postdoc Karina
Banasik and Ph.D. David Westergaard during the course of this project. And I am grate-
ful that you gave me the opportunity to work with this exciting project.
I would also like to thank my ofﬁce mates: Isabella, Francesco and Alex for providing
good discussions and valuable inputs on a daily basis. And for helping me keeping the
mood up on long ofﬁce days.
Special thanks to Kristoffer Burgdorf for providing data from the Danish Blood donor
study, and giving me insight in the studies of DBDS. And thanks to the blood donors
giving consent to use their blood samples for research.
Thanks to postdoc Alexander Junge for good and insightful discussion on machine learn-
ing topics.
Thanks to professor Søren Brunak, for giving me the opportunity to be a part of the Trans-
lational Disease Systems Biology department.
And ﬁnally thanks to all my colleagues at NNF Center for Protein Research for giving me
valuable insight in their ﬁeld of research, and providing a good and educative working
environment.

==PAGE:19==
1
Chapter 1
Introduction
In recent years, several studies have found associations between several common pato-
gens and psychiatric, neurological, and cognitive deﬁcits.
Latent infection with Tox-
oplasma gondi has been associated with schizophrenia, depression, anxiety, Parkinson,
Alzheimers, increased risk of trafﬁc accidents, personality disorders, self-directed vio-
lence and suicide [16][58][49][43][25][57]. T. gondii-infections have a world prevalence
of approximately 30% [34]. Bizarre ﬁndings have been made in T.gondii-infected rodent,
showing infected rats to be attracted to cats odors [36][79][5]. As rats are preyed by cats,
the normal innate defence of the rats are to avoid cat odors. Felines are the deﬁnitive host
of the parasite, why the parasite is considered to manipulate the host for its own beneﬁt.
Making it more likely for the rodent to be preyed upon by cats [36].
T. gondii is a intracellular protozoan parasite [65].
The parasite can infect all warm-
blooded animals.
However, it can only sexually reproduce in the intestine of feline
animals. The main pathway of T. gondii-infection in humans comes from ingestion of
oocystes spread from faeces of infected cats, eating undercooked meat of infected ani-
mals or drinking and eating oocyst contaminated water and vegetables [74]. Further-
more, some studies have shown that infection is transmitted when receiving blood trans-
fusions from T. gondii-infected donors [34][68].
Upon oral infection the parasite multiply and migrate across the intestinal epithelium
[65]. The parasite starts invading white blood cells which then serve as a "trojan horse"
that facilitate the further transport of the parasite through the host [33]. The parasite dis-
seminate though the body and forms parasite-ﬁlled tissue cysts in the muscles, placenta,
or the eyes and brain [54]. Hereby a latent infection in the central nervous system and
body tissue is established [54][23]. The latent T.gondii infection is typically maintained for
life [32]. In latent infected, immune-compromised individuals, the tissue cysts can break,
hereby reactivating the infection, which in some cases lead to inﬂammation of the brain
or eyes [33].
Acquiring the infection during gestation can have tragically outcome for the fetus. Con-
genitally infected children may suffer from diminished vision (retinochoroiditis), growth
failure, hydrocephalus, convulsions and intra-cerebral calciﬁcation [34][40]. Furthermore
some studies have found T. gondii to be an increased risk factor for miscarriage [31].
T. Gondii-infections are diagnosed by either ﬁnding direct evidens of the parasite in body
ﬂuid and tissues or by showing presence of antibodies against the parasite. This is mainly
obtained by a polymerase chain reaction (PCR) or an enzyme-linked immunosorbent as-
say (ELISA). As of yet, there is no routine screening of T. gondii and the available diag-
nostic methods are all high-cost, low-throughput methods [65].
Through applying various machine learning methods, I will attempt to predict the pres-
ence of antibodies against T. gondii in blood donors from the Danish Blood Donor Study
==PAGE:20==
2
Chapter 1. Introduction
(DBDS), in which a subset of donors (N=3973) have been analysed for antibodies against
T. gondii, using an ELISA [42]. Due to the behaviour of the parasite, it is well-founded
to examine if a signal of infection exists in the blood samples routinely obtained at the
blood banks, such as white blood cell count, C-Reactive protein levels, or red blood cell
size. Hereby, a low-cost, high-throughput diagnostic method against T. gondii-infections
could be established. This could potentially lead to a new screening method for T.gondii-
infections and prevent possible transmission of infections from blood transfusions, if im-
plemented at the blood banks.
Many studies have examined possible associations between T. gondii-infections and var-
ious diseases. Nonetheless, no previous studies have tried to predict the presence of T.
gondii from blood samples, thus making this an unique study.
Hypothesis: Latent T. gondii-infections in blood donors can be predicted from blood mea-
surements routinely obtained at the Danish blood banks by training various machine
learning algorithms.
==PAGE:21==
3
Chapter 2
Theory
2.1
Toxoplasma Gondii
FIGURE 2.1: Toxoplasma Gondii parasite. (adapted form AJ Cann via Wiki-
media Commons)
Toxoplasma gondii is an intracellular protozoan parasite of the family Phylum Apicom-
plexa [32]. The parasite is crescent-shaped (ﬁgure 2.1) and holds an approximate size of
2-6 µm in width and 4-8 µm in length [32]. The front part contains secretory organelles
named apical complex which are used for invasion of the host. The locomotion of the
parasite is done by gliding motility allowing the parasite to migrate through tissues and
across biological barriers during host cell invasion [32].
The parasite has three life cycle stages: tachyzoites, sporozoites, and bradyzoites. The
sporozoites are a highly infectious form contained in small oocysts. The tachyzoites are a
fast replicating form that disseminate through the host upon infection. The bradyzoites
are a slow replicating form existing in tissue cysts during the latent phase of infection.
2.1.1
Deﬁnitive host and reproduction
Cats and other felines are the deﬁnitive hosts of the parasite. It is only possible for the
parasite to sexually reproduce in epithelial cells of the small intestine in felines. Thus all
other hosts are intermediate hosts of the parasite.
During reproduction sporozoites-ﬁlled oocyst are formed and hereafter spread to the
environment through feces. All hosts can become infected by digestion of the parasite
==PAGE:22==
4
Chapter 2. Theory
in all its forms. For felines, infection typically occur when they eat infected prey such as
rodents and birds [32]. Infected felines excrete up to 10 million oocystes during one day,
for up to 2 weeks after infection [32]. The oocysts can remain infective up to 1 year in soil
[17].
2.1.2
Transmission route
The main pathway for human infection with T. gondii is considered to be through diges-
tion of contaminated food and water. For instance, eating uncooked meat of infected
animals, unwashed raw vegetables and fruits or using the same cutlery for cutting in-
fected meat and vegetables or drinking contaminated water [74]. Infection can also occur
by contact with feces of infected cats but due to the short period of oocyst shredding,
contact with domestic cats are not thought to be the main pathway of infection [20]. Fur-
thermore some studies have found infection to be transmitted through blood transfusions
or organ transplantation from infected donors [68][34][32].
Upon oral infection with oocysts or bradyzoite-ﬁlled tissue cysts, parasites are released
from the cysts initializing the acute phase of infection. The parasite multiply and be-
come tachyzoites which migrate across the intestinal epithelium into the lamina propria
[65]. The parasites then proceeds invading monocytes, neutrophills, and dendritic cells
[33]. These leukocytes serve as a "trojan horse" that facilitate the further transport of the
parasite through the host [13]. It is still unclear how the parasite enters circulation but T.
gondii are found either as extracellular tachyzoites or inside host cells in the blood shortly
after oral infection in mice [33].
The parasite disseminate though the body and localize in muscle tissue or the placenta
of pregnant women. Furthermore it crosses the blood-brain-barrier (BBB) and the ocular
barriers and localize in the eyes and brain [54]. The parasite then forms bradyzoite-ﬁlled
tissue cysts in the skeletal muscles and neuronal tissue of the host, due to immune pres-
sure [54]. Hereby a latent infection in the central nervous system and body tissue is
established [54][23]. A latent T. gondii infection is typically maintained for life [32].
2.1.3
Epidemiology
It is estimated that 30 % of the worlds population have a latent infection with T. gondii,
developed countries having lower incidence than underdeveloped countries [65]. In Den-
mark the prevalence is approximately 23% [42]. The seroprevalence for infected cats vary
with climate but is about 30% to 40% [32].
So far, no human vaccine for preventing toxoplasmosis exists [34]. However drugs for
treating schizophrenia and bipolar disorders have been shown to inhibit in vitro replica-
tion of T. gondii [38].
2.1.4
Behavioural changes in infected animals
Several studies have found T. gondii-infections in rodents to cause behavioural changes
which make them more likely to be preyed by cats [79]. It has been shown that while
uninfected rats show strong aversion towards cat odour, infected rats not only reduced
their cat-aversion when exposed to cat odour, but actually preferred cat-treated areas
[36][79][5]. How the parasite alters rodents behaviour is unknown but it is assumed
to be a mechanism for enhancing transmission to its deﬁnitive host. This is named the
"parasite manipulation hypothesis". The same mechanism have been found in T. gondii-
infected chimpanzees being more attracted to urine odour from their feline predator, the
==PAGE:23==
2.1. Toxoplasma Gondii
5
leopard. T. gondii-infected men have also been found to rate cat urine odour as more
pleasant compared to ratings from non-infected men [27]. However, contradictory, in-
fected women were found to have a decreased attractiveness towards cat odour [27]. Fur-
thermore observational studies have found T. gondii-infected mice to have signiﬁcantly
decreased learning capacity and memory compared to non-infected mice [79].
2.1.5
T. Gondii-infection and disease associations
If a T. gondii-infection is acquired during gestation, the parasite can cross the placenta
and cause toxoplasmosis in the fetus [40]. Congenitally infected children may suffer from
hearing and vision abnormalities (retinochoroiditis), hydrocephalus, seizures and calciﬁ-
cations in the central nervous system. The severity of toxoplasmosis in the child depends
on at what point in pregnancy the mother was exposed [34][40]. Furthermore, some
studies have found T. gondii to be an increased risk factor for miscarriage [31].
Immune compromised individuals such as those being HIV-infected or undergoing can-
cer treatment, may also experience serious side-effects from a latent T. gondii-infection.
Due to a suppressed immune system the bradyzoite-ﬁlled cysts can break, hereby reac-
tivating infection. This may lead to inﬂammation of the brain (encephalitis) or of eyes
(retinochorioditis)[33].
Besides these severe manifestations of infection, several studies have found associations
between latent T. gondii-infections and several psychiatric, neurological and cognitive
deﬁcits. Subjects having a latent T. gondii-infection have been found to have a higher
risk of attempting and committing suicide [37][57]. A Danish study found T. gondii-
infected mothers to have a 2-fold higher probability of committing suicide and 1.5-fold
higher probability for suicide attempts compared to non-infected mothers [57]. It has also
been shown that a latent T. gondii-infection increases the risk of developing schizophre-
nia [58][75]. In one study the risk of developing schizophrenia was found to be increased
about 2.7 times for infected subjects. This increase is much higher than any increased risk
being related to schizophrena-associated genes [75].
Furthermore, elevated T. gondii seroprevalance have been found in people with a history
of bipolar disorder [56][15] and patients with obsessive-compulsive-disorder (OCD) [50],
when compared to healthy controls . Subjects with latent toxoplasmosis have also been
found to have a signiﬁcantly increased risk of trafﬁc accidents [25]. Moreover latent T.
gondii-infection has been found to be associated with depression [37], Parkinson’s disease
[49], Alzheimer’s disease [43], and personality disorders [35].
2.1.6
Transmission from blood transfusion
In many papers T. gondii are stated to be transmitted through blood transfusion and organ
transplantations [34][2][19]. However, only few studies on the subject have been made
[68][64]. Nonetheless, patients undergoing organ transplantations are often immune-
suppressed. Therefore, acquiring a T.gondii-infection in this state can have severe con-
sequences. Furthermore, blood transfusions are also often given to immune-suppressed
patients such as cancer patients [78].
Even though T.gondii may transmit through blood transfusion it seems that the possible
side-effects of neurodegenerative disorders does not transmit. A study from 2016 in-
vestigated if neurodegenerative disorders is transmitted through blood transfusions and
found no evidence here-off [18].
==PAGE:24==
6
Chapter 2. Theory
2.1.7
Diagnosis of T. gondii-infection
T. gondii-infection can be diagnosed by a polymerase chain reaction (PCR) where pres-
ence of the parasites DNA is sought. Diagnosis can also be made from serological meth-
ods by testing the blood for antibodies against T. gondii. This can for instance be achieved
through an enzyme-linked immunosorbent essay (ELISA) [45]. It can be hard to distin-
guish acute infection from latent infection, however the presence of speciﬁc T. gondii IgG-
antibodies indicate that the subject has been infected at some point in the past [34][65].
2.1.8
Possible signal of infection from basic blood measurements
The parasite has been shown to circulate in the blood of both acute and chronically
infected subjects. These ﬁndings have been made in both immune-compromised and
immune-competent subjects, regardless of their T. gondii-caused inﬂammation history
[69]. The tachyzoites have also been found to invade leucocytes, preferentially, mono-
cytes and dendritic cells over other peripheral blood leucocytes [10][33]. Furthermore,
the parasite has been shown to penetrate red blood cells in a in-vitro study [73]. Due
to these ﬁndings a signal of infection could exists in the blood measurements regularly
obtained at the Danish blood donation clinics.
As for now, T. gondii-infection is only diagnosed by the high-cost, low-throughout meth-
ods described in section 2.1.7. Furthermore, no regular blood-screenings towards T. gondii
are made at the blood banks of Denmark.
==PAGE:25==
2.2. Machine Learning
7
2.2
Machine Learning
The primary goal in machine learning is to construct models which generalise to unseen
data based on patterns learned from training data. In classiﬁcation problems, a pattern
which can discriminate between speciﬁc classes are sought. In a medical setting this
pattern could be of disease/no-disease. However, machine learning methods are used in
many other ﬁelds.
2.2.1
Training, testing, and over-ﬁtting
Before training the algorithms, the data is to be separated into two datasets called a test-
and training-set. The algorithm is only allowed to see and learn from the training-set.
When the model is fully trained it is afterwards tested on the test-set to evaluate how
well it performs on unseen data, and thus evaluate if a generic model has been trained.
If the algorithm achieves good performance on the training-data, but poor results on the
test-set, it indicates that the model is over-ﬁtting. That is, the algorithm has ﬁtted the
training-data so well, that it has learned a very speciﬁc pattern of just that dataset. How-
ever it has not learned a generic model, and therefore it will perform poorly when used
on unseen data. It is therefore desired to obtain about the same performance on both the
test- and training-set.
When splitting the data, it is very important to balance the split according to the pro-
portions between classes. If eg. after splitting randomly the training-set would contain
only a single observation of a speciﬁc class and the test-set would contain the remaining
observations of that class, it would be impossible for the algorithm to learn a pattern of
that class, when only having the data of a single observation to learn from. Thus a useless
classiﬁer would be obtained. Therefore, when working with unbalanced dataset, splits
has to be stratiﬁed so that the class-distribution are about the same in both the training-
and test-set.
2.2.2
Hyper-parameter tuning
Each classiﬁer has different settings which can be changed in order to optimize the per-
formance of the classiﬁer. These settings are part of the architecture of the algorithms and
are called hyper-parameters. For instance, for a regularized logistic regression the regu-
larization penalty C has to be selected (section 2.2.6). In this case, C is a hyper-parameter.
When implementing a machine learning algorithm it is very important to try-out differ-
ent hyper-parameter settings as this can have a considerable impact on the performance
of the classiﬁer. Finding the optimal settings can be done manually by try-and-error or
automatically using grid-search or random-search with cross-validation.
2.2.3
Cross-validation
K-fold cross-validation is a statistical method used to evaluate the generalization perfor-
mance of eg. a classiﬁer by partitioning the data in k-folds (ﬁgure 2.2).
FIGURE 2.2: 5-fold cross-validation split (adapted from [53])
==PAGE:26==
8
Chapter 2. Theory
In each cross-validation loop a model is trained. As illustrated in ﬁgure 2.2, the ﬁrst
model is trained using the ﬁrst fold as test-data and the remaining as training-data, the
second model uses the second fold as test-data and so on. Hence a 5-fold cross-validation
loop leads to 5 models. A generalized classiﬁer performance can be obtained by calculat-
ing the mean and standard-deviation of the performance scores obtained by the models
of each loop. By using the same hyper-parameters in all loops, cross-validation can be
used to get a robust estimate of how a speciﬁc set of hyper-parameters performs.
Before running cross-validation, the whole dataset is to be separated into a test- and
training-set as described in section 2.2.1. Cross-validation with a speciﬁc set of hyper-
parameters is then run on only the training-data, thus a test-set within the training-set
is obtained in each cross-validation loop. This test-set is called the validation-set (ﬁgure
2.3).
FIGURE 2.3: Splitting when optimizing hyper-parameters (adapted from
[53])
By running cross-validation for different combinations of hyper-parameters settings, the
settings obtaining the best mean performance on the validation-sets can be found. Here-
after a classiﬁer using the best performing hyper-parameter-settings can be trained us-
ing both the training- and validation-set. This ﬁnal model is then to run on the test-set,
to evaluate its performance. Changing the hyper-parameters settings for many cross-
validations can be done manually or automatic using grid-search or random-search.
Grid-search
Grid-search is an exhaustive search through a speciﬁed hyper-parameter space. By grid-
search all possible combinations of the hyper-parameter space are tried. Even though the
process can run in parallel it is very computational expensive.
Random-search
It has been shown that random search is far more efﬁcient for hyper-parameter search
than grid-search [6]. Random search obtains an equal or better performance than grid-
search in a fraction of the computational time [6]. Where grid-search tries all combina-
tions in the hyper-parameter space, random-search randomly selects a combination of
hyper-parameters from a hyper-parameter space deﬁned by distributions, arrays of spe-
ciﬁc values or a combination here-off. The difference between the two hyper-parameter
spaces is shown for two features in ﬁgure 2.4.
==PAGE:27==
2.2. Machine Learning
9
FIGURE 2.4: Grid search and random search (adapted from [6]). The op-
timal hyper-parameter setting for the important and unimportant hyper-
parameter in green and the yellow, respectively.
In ﬁgure 2.4, it is seen that random-search tries more different settings for the important
parameter (x-axis) than grid-search, and is therefore more efﬁcient.
Stratiﬁed cross-validation
As mentioned in section 2.2.1 it is crucial to obtain same class distribution in the test- and
training-data. The same goes for the each fold in the cross-validation loop. The problem
rising when not taking the class distribution into account is illustrated in ﬁgure 2.5.
FIGURE 2.5:
Non-stratiﬁed cross-validation (top) and stratiﬁed cross-
validation (bottom) with 3 class labels (adapted from [53])
Figure 2.5 shows a 3-class classiﬁcation problem where data is sorted according to classes
so that all observations belonging to the same class is positioned together. If the class dis-
tribution is not taken into account when splitting the data, a classiﬁer could be trained on
data only containing class 1 and 2, and therefore not being able to classify class 3. There-
fore, cross-validation should be made on stratiﬁed splits, ensuring same class distribution
in each fold.
Beneﬁts of cross-validation
The beneﬁts of using cross-validation is that more robust results are obtained as the mean
performance of each cross-validation loops is calculated. Furthermore, the whole dataset
is used more efﬁciently as it is split in a speciﬁed number of folds. Moreover by doing
many splits and calculating the standard deviation of the performance for each model,
it is possible to estimate how sensitive the classiﬁers are to the selection of training-data
==PAGE:28==
10
Chapter 2. Theory
[53].
The disadvantage of cross-validation is the computational costs associated with training
k models.
2.2.4
Model evaluation
In order to evaluate the performance of the classiﬁers, different evaluation metrics can be
used. Which metric to choose is very much dependent on the classiﬁcation problem and
the dataset. In binary-classiﬁcation problems, with imbalanced datasets, the receiver op-
erating characteristics (ROC) and the area under the ROC-curve (AUC) is often used as
a performance score. This metric is chosen because the ROC-curve and its AUC is calcu-
lated from a combination of both the true positive rate (eq. 2.2) and the false positive rate
(eq. 2.1) [22], hereby making it insensitive to class-skewness [61][24]. Furthermore, ROC-
curve contribute with information of what performances can be achieved by changing
the probability-thresholds of a classiﬁer.
ROC-curve
ROC graphs are two-dimensional, plotting the false-positive rate (FPR) on the x-axis and
the true-positive rate (TRP) on the y-axis. An example is shown in ﬁgure 2.6.
FIGURE 2.6: Example of a ROC-curve (adapted from scikit-learn.org)
The metrics used to construct the ROC curve are shown in equation 2.1, 2.2.
TPR = recall =
TP
TP + FP = TP
P
(2.1)
FPR = 1 −speciﬁcity = 1 −TN

=
FP
FP + TN = FP
N
(2.2)
==PAGE:29==
2.2. Machine Learning
11
FP being the number of false positives, TP the number of true positives, P the total num-
ber of positives, TN the number of true negatives and N the total number of negatives.
A random classiﬁer will obtain a ROC-score which slides up and down on the diagonal
according to the frequency of which it randomly guesses the classes [22]. Therefore the
diagonal line y = x in the ROC graph represents random-guessing.
In order for the classiﬁer to obtain a ROC-curve above the random guessing line, it needs
to exploit patterns in the data. The area under the ROC-curve of a random classiﬁer is
0.5, hence a classiﬁer with an AUC score higher than 0.5 performs better than random
guessing. From ﬁgure 2.6 it is seen that the best obtainable ROC-curve is a straight line
from zero to one on both axis, obtaining an AUC of 1.0. Thus 1.0 is the highest achievable
AUC score.
More scoring metrics
In order to properly evaluate the performance of a classiﬁer, using different metrics is
helpful as a broader impression of the classiﬁers performance is obtained. A few more of
the metrics used to evaluate the models in this study are presented.

TP + FP
(2.3)
f1score = 2 · precision · recall

The lowest and highest score for precision, recall, f1-score and accuracy are 0 and 1, re-
spectively. Matthews corr. coef. (MCC) ranges from -1 to 1. -1 being complete disagree-
ments between observations and predictions, 0 is the same as random guessing, and +1
represents perfect prediction. MCC is regarded as a balanced measure as it combines all
part of the confusion matrix [8].
2.2.5
Classiﬁcation
In binary classiﬁcations we wish to distinguish between two classes. The output of a
binary-classiﬁer should be the probability of belonging to one of the classes. By choosing
a probability-threshold, observations can be assigned to either class, based on the proba-
bility output of the classiﬁer. The variable, y, to predict is therefore deﬁned in the range
0 ≤y ≤1
In the following a brief description of the machine learning algorithms used in this study
are outlined.
2.2.6
Logistic Regression
A logistic regression corresponds to ﬁtting the model shown in equation 2.7 [4].

This implies that if the true class is 1 that is y = 1 and the model predicts the data-point
x(i) as having a probability of 1.0 for belonging to class 1 the cost for this classiﬁcation will
be zero as log(hΘ(x(i))) = log(1.0) = 0. However if the classiﬁer outputs a probability
lower than 1.0 the cost for this classiﬁcation will be negative. The opposite implies when
y = 0.
Therefore the goal when training the logistic regression algorithm is to ﬁt the parame-
ters θ as to maximize the log-likelihood. This can be done using various optimization
algorithms here among gradient ascent.
Gradient ascent
By gradient ascent we walk in steps of size η (learning rate) in the direction of the gradi-
ent of the log-likelihood function that maximizes the log-likelihood. The gradient is the
partial derivative of the log-likelihood with respect to each of the weights, θ, deﬁned by
equation 2.15 [4]:
∇θL =
N
X
i=1
(y(i) −hΘ(x(i)))x(i)
(2.15)
And updating the weights θ is then done by equation 2.16 :
θnew = θ + η∇θL
(2.16)
Regularization
In order to ﬁnd a model that generalizes well and thus avoid over-ﬁtting, regularisation
can be applied to logistic regression. The two most popular types of regularization are
Ridge Regression (L2 regularization) and Lasso regularization (L1).
The regularization term R(θ) are subtracted from the log-likelihood hereby penalizing
huge weights where L1 and L2 regularization is deﬁned by:
L1 : R(θ) = C
N
X
i=1
| θ(i) |
(2.17)
L2 : R(θ) = C
N
X
i=1
(θ(i))2
(2.18)
The hyper-parameter C controls the amount of regularization that is applied. High C
values corresponding to a lot of regularization and low values to less regularization. L1
penalization is a stronger regularizer than L2 and more sparse feature weights are ob-
tained when using L1 over L2.
==PAGE:32==
14
Chapter 2. Theory
We do not wish to regularize the bias therefore the bias θ(0) = b and the feature weights
are updated separately during gradient ascent.
Feature importance
Part from preventing over-ﬁtting, applying regularization to logistic regression can also
be useful for determine which features of the dataset are most important in terms of
classiﬁcation. This is because, when applying regularization (especially L1) more sparse
solutions are sought. Hereby, only the most important features in terms of classiﬁcation
are allowed to be included in the model. The magnitude of the model coefﬁcients can
therefore be used to gain an insight of how much each feature contributes to classiﬁcation.
2.2.7
Random Forest Classiﬁer
A random forest classiﬁer is an example of an ensemble method where many estimators
are aggregated in order to achieve better performance. Random forest is an ensemble of
decision trees.
Decision trees
Decision trees essentially learn a system of if/else questions leading to a decision [53]. An
example of a simple decision tree classifying circles and triangles is illustrated in ﬁgure
2.8.
FIGURE 2.8: A simpel decison tree solving a binary classiﬁcation problem
using 2 features, x and y. (Adapted from [72])
The top node which has no incoming edges is called the "root node". Nodes which has
exactly one incoming edge and two or more outgoing edges are called "internal nodes".
For the decision tree illustrated in ﬁgure 2.8 these are the nodes asking "y<0.47? and
"y<0.33?". And the last nodes in the tree, each having exactly one incoming edge and no
outgoing edges are called "leaf nodes" [72].
It is computational infeasible to ﬁnd the optimal tree, as exponentially many decision
trees can be build from a set of features [72]. However the optimal solution is approxi-
mated by building a decision tree using a greedy algorithm that decides which features
==PAGE:33==
2.2. Machine Learning
15
to use in each split by making locally optimum decisions. This is eg. done by Hunt’s al-
gorithm which is a recursive algorithm that constructs a leaf of class label yt if all records
in the node belongs to the same class yt. If the node contains records belonging to more
than one class, a split is made. This split is made using the gain, ∆, which is a criterion
that determines the goodness of a split [72]. The gain can be estimated using different
impurity measures. The two most commonly used impurity measures are Entropy and
Gini (equation 2.19 and 2.20).
Entropy(t) = −
c−1
X
i=0
p(i|t)log2(p(i|t))
(2.19)
Gini(t) = 1 −
c−1
X
i=0
[p(i|t)]2
(2.20)
Where c is the number of classes and p(i|t) is the fraction of observations belonging to
class i at node t [72]. The gain is a measure of the impurity before and after splitting, the
larger the difference the better. The gain is calculated as shown in equation 2.21:
∆= I(parent) −
k
X
j=1
N(vj)
N
I(vj)
(2.21)
I(·) being the impurity measure of a given node, N the total number of datapoints at the
parent node, k the number of features and N(vj) the number of data-points going into
the child-node vj [72].
Random Forest
Decision tress are intuitive easy to understand. However, one of its major drawbacks is
that they tend to over-ﬁt. This can be avoided by implementing pre- and post-pruning,
hereby setting restrictions on the architecture of the tree. However ﬁnding the right prun-
ing settings can require much hyper-parameter tuning.
What has proven to work well instead is using the random forest algorithm [53]. The
random forest classiﬁer works by building many different decision trees and averaging
their results. Each tree used for averaging should do a decent job at predicting the right
class, and be slightly different from one an other. While each decision tree overﬁt, aver-
aging their results will generate a generic model [53]. It is very important that each trees
is slightly different as averaging over the same model wont improve performance.
The trees are built using random choices insuring that they will be distinct. First of all
each tree is built on a bootstrap sample of the data. Data-points are drawn randomly
with replacement from the training-set until a dataset of the same size as the training-set
is obtained. In this new dataset some data-points of the original will be missing, while
others will be repeated. By building a tree on such dataset, a slightly different tree will be
built each time. Another important setting for obtaining distinct trees is that a maximum
number of features is allowed to be used for building each tree. The features that are
allowed to be used are sampled randomly without replacement when building each tree.
The number of trees to use in a random forest depend on the computational power avail-
able thus more tress generally lead to more generic models [53].
In order to further optimized the performance of a random forest, pruning of the deci-
sion trees can also be implemented. A few examples could be to put restrictions on the
==PAGE:34==
16
Chapter 2. Theory
maximum depth and number of features to be used, or requiring a minimum number of
data-points when splitting a node.
Feature importance
The most important features used by a trained random forest classiﬁer for a given clas-
siﬁcation task, can be estimated using the impurity measures (equation 2.19, 2.20). This
is done by computing how much each feature decreases the weighted impurity in a tree
and averaging over all tress in the random forest. Features can then be ranked according
to this measure [70].
2.2.8
Neural Networks
Neutral networks are a branch of models inspired by the nervous system of the human
body. The human brain is made up of a large network of thousands of connected neurons.
In the same way deep neural networks are built of a large number of nodes connected in
a large network. Each node in the neural network can be modelled in the same way as a
logistic regression model (described in section 2.2.6) where a sigmoid function is applied
to the sum of the the weighted inputs. One node of a neural network is illustrated in
ﬁgure 2.9.
FIGURE 2.9: One neuron of a neural network (adapted from [9])
Where x = [x1, x2..xn] are the inputs and w = [w1, w2.., wn] are the weights of the model.
Just as for logistic regression a bias term, b, is also included in the model, however it is not
displayed in the ﬁgure. All the weighted inputs are summed together in the node and an,
often non-linear, activation function, f(·), is applied to the sum. This activation function
can be a sigmoid function leading to a logistic regression model, but different activation
functions can be applied, all having slightly different characteristics. The output of the
neuron can then be expressed by equation 2.22:
y = f(x · wT + b)
(2.22)
The output is sent to other neurons and a neural network of connected neurons can be
made. And example of such a network is shown in ﬁgure 2.10 where each node corre-
sponds to a linear model deﬁned by equation 2.22.
==PAGE:35==
2.2. Machine Learning
17
FIGURE 2.10: Example of a feed-forward neural network with 4 inputs and
2 hidden layers of 3 nodes each (adapted from [53])
The neurons are called nodes and each layer of nodes are called hidden layers except
the last layer which is the output-layer. The network can be build in various ways using
different number of nodes and layers. Deciding on the architecture is part of hyper-
parameter tuning of the model.
Activation functions
The most widely used activation functions are the sigmoid, the hyperbolic tangent func-
tion (tanh) and the rectiﬁed linear unit (ReLU) [9]. The tanh-function is shaped similarly
to the S-shape of the sigmoid function (see 2.7) however its output ranges from -1 to
1 instead of from 0 to 1. The ReLU-function is deﬁned as: f(z) = max(0, z) having a
characteristic hockey-stick-shaped look as illustrated in ﬁgure 2.11
FIGURE 2.11: The ReLU activation function (adapted from [9])
The output layer
For binary-classiﬁcation problems the output of the neural network should be a proba-
bility of seeing class 1. As for the logistic regression (eq 2.7), this is achieved by applying
a sigmoid function to the output layer.
==PAGE:36==
18
Chapter 2. Theory
Back-propagation
Back-propagation is a method used in neural networks for ﬁnding the gradient of a spec-
iﬁed cost-function with respect to the weights in each layer. After ﬁnding the gradient, a
gradient decent method can be used to update the weights in each layer.
Just as for the logistic regression a cost function, J(w), of the output can be deﬁned. It
is common to update the weights of a neural network by gradient-decent methods, thus
the cost function is deﬁned as the negative log-likelihood shown in equation 2.23.
J(w) = −
N
X
i=1
y(i)log(ˆy(i)) + (1 −y(i))log(1 −ˆy(i))
(2.23)
Where y(i) is the true class label and ˆy is the output of the last layer of the neural network
corresponding to the output hθ(x) of the logistic regression deﬁned in section 2.2.6. This
negative log-likelihood is sometimes referred to as the cross entropy error [66].
When ﬁnding the gradient with respect to the weights of a speciﬁc layer the error is
propagated backwards form the output layer. Thus by using the chain rule (equation
2.24) the gradient with respect to the weights can be found. Given h(x) = f(g(x)) the
chain rule states that the derivative of h(x) can be found by equation 2.24.
dh
dx = df
dg
dg
dx
(2.24)
For instance, the gradient with respect to the weights connecting the hidden units to the
output layer is found by equation 2.25 [66].
∂J

Where h(j) is the output of the last hidden layer, ˆy(i) the true class. Finding the gradi-
ent with respect to weight of a lower level in the network require more applications of
the chain-rule [66]. In backprogration the gradients are found by dynamic programming
where the gradient of one hidden layer will be used to compute the gradient of the layer
below.
Next the weights can be updated using various gradient-based optimization algorithms.
Regularization of neural networks
One of the central problems with neural networks is that of over-ﬁtting, just as it is for the
other machine learning algorithms explained. There exists many forms of regularization
which can be applied to neural networks, I will cover just a few.
Dropout
Dropout is a regularization technique where the neurons, during training, are only kept
active with some speciﬁed probability, p. This prevent the network from becoming to
==PAGE:37==
2.2. Machine Learning
19
dependent on any neurons or combinations here-off [9]. This is illustrated in ﬁgure 2.12
where the connections to some nodes are cut during one round of training.
FIGURE 2.12: Dropout illustrated (adapted from [9])
Applying dropout can also be viewed as approximately combining exponentially many
different neural networks [9]. The probability p is a hyper-parameter which require tun-
ing.
L1/L2-regularization
Just as for logistic regression the weights of a neural network can be regularized using L1
or L2 regularization (see section 2.2.6), L2 being the most commonly used [9]. Instead of
regularizing all weights in the network, regularization can be applied to only the weights
of speciﬁc layers. L1 and L2 regularization work as described in section 2.2.6
Training a neural network
When training a neural network, in addition to the hyper-parameters relating to the ar-
chitecture and regularization of the network, at least two more hyper-parameters has to
be set. These are the number of epochs and the batch size.
An epoch is when the training-data is run through the network once. As updating the
weights is a iterative process the training-data needs to run through the neural network
several times. Therefore the number of epochs deﬁne how long the neural network is
trained.
Batch size is a term rising from the fact that training-data often is of a size which can-
not pass through the network all at once. Therefore training-data needs to be split into
batches. The number of training records in a single batch is called the batch size.
Early stopping
Early stopping is a kind of regularization method where training is stopped when the
model starts over-ﬁtting the training data. The error on the validation-set is used as a
indicator of over-ﬁtting. Hereby, training can be stopped as soon as the error on the
validation-set increases, however other stopping thresholds can be used [3].
==PAGE:38==
20
Chapter 2. Theory
2.3
Pre-processing
Some machine learning algorithms require pre-processing of the data in order to work.
For instance, the logistic regression and the neural network cannot work with categorical
features. Furthermore they expect the features to be standardized.
Feature scaling
The regularized logistic regression and the neural network are very sensitive to the scal-
ing of the data. Different scaling techniques can be applied to the data here among, stan-
dard scaling, robust scaling, and minimum-maximum scaling. These scaling techniques
are brieﬂy explained in the following.
Standard-scaling: Features are standardized by removing the mean and scaling to unit
variance. Hereby all features obtain a mean of zero and a variance of one. This transfor-
mation is done by equation 2.27
z = x −µ
σ
(2.27)
z being the scaled feature, µ the mean and σ the standard-deviation of the feature.
Robust scaling: Robust-scaling is very similar to standard-scaling, however instead the
median is subtracted and quantiles are used instead of the variance. This scaling tech-
nique is more robust to outliers, and therefore used when many outliers are present in
the data. The scaling is shown in equation 2.28.
z =
x −Q1(x)
Q3(x) −Q1(x)
(2.28)
Q1 being the 1st quantile and Q3 being the 3rd-quantile of the feature.
Min-max-scaling: Features are scaled by subtracting the minimum value of the feauture
and dividing by the span between the maximum and the minimum value of that features
as shown in equation 2.29. Min-max-scaling lead to values in [0, 1].
z =
x −min(x)
max(x) −min(x)
(2.29)
One-hot encoding
Many classiﬁers cannot work with categorical features. This can be solved by hot-key
encoding. By one-hot encoding, a new binary feature for each category in the original
feature is made. An alternative to one-hot-encoding is using numbers to encode categor-
icals. However, it is not always desired to view categories as having a hierarchy, thus
which encoding to use depends on the dataset and on the learning algorithm to be used.
2.4
Lab-value-imputation
Missing data is a common problem when working with many data types. As the machine
learning algorithms previously described cannot work with missing data, imputation is
necessary.
==PAGE:39==
2.4. Lab-value-imputation
21
2.4.1
missForest algorithm
MissForest has proved to be a highly accurate imputation method when applied to many
different datasets including laboratory data [76]. One big advantage of missForest is that
it can handle both categorical and continuous data.
As the name indicates, missForest uses random forest to impute missing values.
In order to explain the algorithm easier, some deﬁnitions are made.
For an arbitrary feature Xs from dataset X, with missing values at entries i(s)
mis ∈1, .., n we
can separated the dataset X into 4 parts [51].
• The observed values of feature Xs, denoted y(s)
obs
• The missing values of Xs, denoted y(s)
mis
• The features other than Xs, not including the observations having a missing value
in Xs, denoted x(s)
obs.
• The features other than Xs, with observations having a missing value in Xs, denoted
x(s)
mis
An example of separating a random dataset X by X6 can be seen in ﬁgure 2.13.
FIGURE 2.13:
Example of a dataset X, separated into 4 parts for X6
(adapted from [51]).
The missForest algorithm works as follows: First an initial guess of the missing values
in the data X is made. This can for instance be done by mean imputation. Next all
features in X is sorted in ascending order according to how many missing values they
contain. Hereafter, the missing values of each feature y(s)
mis in X are imputed by ﬁtting a
random forest to x(s)
obs using y(s)
obs as the target variable. The algorithm is then applied to
x(s)
mis hereby predicting the missing values y(s)
mis [71]. This procedure is repeated until a
stopping criteria is met.
The stopping criterion is reached when the difference between the newest imputed dataset
and the previous dataset increases for the ﬁrst time [71]. This goes for both continues, N,
and categorical variables, F . The difference for both variable types are deﬁned by equa-
tion 2.30 and 2.31 [71].

Chapter 3
Data description
The data has been collected from blood donors who signed up to participate in the Dan-
ish Blood Donor Study (DBDS). The DBDS is a national large-scale prospective cohort of
Danish blood donors including more than 110.000 Danish individuals between 18 and
67 years. The DBDS holds EDTA plasma, DNA, consecutive samples for all individuals,
questionnaires and register data [42].
For a selected subset of donors in the DBDS (N=3973) their blood samples from 2010 have
been tested for C-reactive protein and antibodies against infections of Cytomegalovirus,
Herpes simplex virus-1 and Toxoplasma gondii. This subset of donors are used in this study.
Furthermore their blood samples have been analysed as by the normal procedures at the
Danish blood banks hereby obtaining a complete blood count of the donors (section 3.2).
In addition to the available clinical measures, the blood donors have also been asked to
complete a questionnaire regarding their well-being, daily-life and food consumption,
ect. The answers to a selection of these questions are also included in this study. Fur-
thermore the donors history of either suicide attempts, trafﬁcs accidents, or psychiatric
illness are included.
3.1
Blood donor regulations
To ensure high quality blood and avoid transmission of disease through blood transfu-
sions, blood donors are subject to strict regulations. These regulations regard certain
diseases, blood deﬁciency, drug/medication use, weight, recent travels and more. If a
donor fails to meet the requirements, blood donation is not allowed. Therefore, all blood
donors are considered to be healthy individuals at the time of donation.
3.2
The complete blood count
The blood tests made at the Danish blood donation clinics capture a wide range of blood
features. This collection of blood measurements are called the complete blood count
(CBC). The CBC is used to evaluate the over-all health of the patient, and to assist in
detection of a wide range of diseases. An overview of the CBC-features included in this
study are shown in table 3.1.

3.2.1
White blood cell indices
WBC: Represents the white blood cell (WBC) concentration. The WBCs are also called
leukocytes. Leukocytes are the cells of the immune system, protecting the body against
infectious disease and foreign invaders. Approximately 1% of the total blood volume is
WBCs in healthy adults. WBCs are divided into ﬁve main types: neutrophils, lympho-
cytes, monocytes, eosinophils and basophils. The number of WBCs are often used as an
indicator of disease however the sensitivity and speciﬁcity is low [77].
Elevation in the leukocyte concentration is called leukocytosis. Leukocytosis is an indi-
cator of infection, inﬂammation or tissue necrosis which can be due to acute heart attack,
leukemia and other disorders [30]. Leukocytosis have also been associated with increased
morality for acute coronary syndrome-patients. Furthermore, leukocytosis can also be a
response to emotional or physical stress such as anxiety, seizures or over-exertion [30].
Speciﬁc increase in eosinophill levels (eosinophilia) are common in parasitic infections
such as a T. gondii-infection [30]. Decreased WBC concentrations are a result of increased
WBC destruction or decreased production of WBCs in the bone marrow. This condition is
called leukopeni. Leukopeni is common in untreated HIV/AIDS patients and cancer pa-
tients receiving chemotherapy. However, HIV/AIDS and cancer patients are not allowed
to donate blood.
3.2.2
Red blood cell indices
RBC: Represents the red blood cell (RBC) concentration. RBCs are also known as ery-
throcytes. Erythrocytes are responsible for delivering oxygen to the body and transport
==PAGE:43==
3.2. The complete blood count
25
CO2 to the lungs for excretion [30]. Erythrocytes contain hemoglobin which is an iron-
containing molecule that binds oxygen. 40-45 % of the bloods volume are RBCs and
approximately 2 million new RBCs are formed per second in adults [30].
HGB: Represents hemoglobin (Hgb) concentration. The function of Hbg is to remove
CO2 from the cells and supply them with oxygen.
Approximately 300 million Hgb
molecules are contained in a single RBC [30]
HCT: Represent the hematocrit of the donor. Hematocrit is the volume percentage of ery-
throcytes relative to the total volume of blood [30]
MCV: Represent the average volume of a RBC. MCV can be used to identify possible
causes of anemia or other diseases [30]. MCV is calculated by equation 3.1 [21].
MCV = HCT
RBC
(3.1)
MCH: Represents the average amount of hemoglobin per RBC. MCH tends to correlate
with MCV because the RBC size is largely dependent on its hemoglobion content. MCH
is calculated by equation 3.2 [21].
MCH = HGB
RBC
(3.2)
MCHC: Represents the average concentration of hemoglobin in a volume of packed
RBCs. Low values meaning lower level of hemoglobin in the RBCs. MCHC is calculated
by equation 3.3 [21].
MCHC = HGB
HCT
(3.3)
Alternations in RBC, HBG or HCT is usually related, therefore increases and decreases of
these features are discussed together.
Increase in RBC (erythrocytosis) can be a response to disease of the bone marrow causing
enhanced production of RBCs. Erythrocytosis is more often a compensatory response
to decrease in oxygen delivery caused by cardiopulmonary diseases. Furthermore dehy-
dration, excessive exercise, anxiety, renal and tumor cancers can also cause erythrocytosis
[30].
Decreases in RBC, HBG or HCT are associated with anemia. Anemia is a decrease in
the number of RBCs or hemoglobin in the blood hereby lowering the bloods ability to
transport oxygen. Anemia can lead to life-threatening complications here among heart
failure. Anemia can be caused by iron, folate and Vitamin B12 deﬁciencies or leukemia,
renal failure, and acute blood loss.
RDW-CV: Red blood cell distribution width coefﬁcient of variation.
RDW-SD: Red blood cell distribution width standard deviation.
The RDW represents a quantitative measure of the variance in erythrocyte size [21]. Ele-
vation in RDW means that the blood cells are of unequal size, this is known as anisocyto-
sis. RDW is used to differentiate between the different causes of anemia [21]. Increase in
RDW have been found to be strongly associated with risk of all-cause mortality, cancer
mortality and chronic lower respiratory tract disease mortality [60]. However, the bio-
logical mechanisms behind greater RDW and mortality risk is unclear.
==PAGE:44==
26
Chapter 3. Data description
3.2.3
Platelet indices
PLT: Represents the platelet concentration. Platelets are also known as thrombocytes. The
main function of thrombocytes are to stop a bleeding when the endothelium has broken.
This is accomplished by platelets clumping together forming clots. There are three main
categories of platelet disorders: thrombocytosis (too many), thrombocytopenia (too few)
and dysfunctional. Thrombocytosis may be caused by cancers, anemia, inﬂammation
or infection. Thrombocytopenia may be caused by medications, certain cancers, kidney
infection/dysfunction or inherited conditions. Furthermore some NSAIDS have the side-
effect of suppressing normal platelet function.
PCT: Represents the plateletcrit, a measure of the ratio of the platelet volume to the whole
blood volume.
P-LCR: Represents the platelet-large cell ratio. P-LCR is a measure of the percentage of
large platelets.
PDW: Represents the platelet size deviation width. The PDW reﬂects the uniformity of
the platelets.
MPV: Represents the mean platelet volume. This is the average size of platelets found in
blood. MPV is a relation between PCT and PLT calculated by equation 3.4.
MPV = PCT
PLT · 105
(3.4)
Platelet indices can be used to assist in determining the function of a persons bone mar-
row [52]. Furthermore the indices are used to evaluate thrombocytic function and in
screening for risk of coronary artery diseases, Alzheimer’s disease, diabetes, inﬂamma-
tory bowel disease and disease of the kidneys [52].
3.2.4
Blood group indices
All cells of the body including the RBCs are covered with antigens [14]. If the immune-
system is presented to a antigen not present on the body’s own cell an immune-response
against the antigen will be initialized. Therefore, it is extremely important to match the
antigens of the blood donor and the recipient. The antigens expressed on the surface of
RBCs are devided into two blood groups, the AB0 and Rhesus blood group.
AB0: There exist four basic AB0 phenotypes. Those are AB, A, B and 0. The immune
system form antibodies towards the AB0 blood groups which are not found on the RBC
surface. This implies that a donor with phenotype A is representing antigen-A on the
surface of their RBCs and form B-antibodies. Phenotype 0 does not have any antigens and
thus both A and B antibodies. Phenotype AB have both AB antigens and no antibodies
[14].
Rhesus: There exist two Rhesus (Rh) phenotypes. Those are the Rh-positive and Rh-
negative. The Rhesus-antigens are contrary to AB0 only presented on the RBCs. Many
different Rhesus-antigens exist however the D-antigen is the most important due to its
high immunogenicity [14].
3.3

All blood donors included in the study were asked questions regarding their well-being,
daily-life, weight and food consumption, amongst others. A selection of the questions
from the questionnaire was included in this study (table 3.2).

3.4
C-reactive protein and infection indices
The blood donors were tested for C-reactive protein (CRP) levels and IgG-antibodies
against cytomegalovirus (CMV), Herpes Simplex Virus-1 (HSV1) and T. gondii infections.
The IgG-antibody levels have been measure for all infections. From the IgG and CRP
levels, z-scores are computed by equation 3.5 using the standard deviation σ and mean
value µ of the speciﬁc batch in which the record was included. A total of 3 batches was
analysed.
The presence of infection was determined by expert in the ﬁeld; Robert Yolken, M.D.1
z = x −µ
σ
(3.5)
Where x is the raw IgG-antibody or CRP level. All indices are presented in table 3.3.

University, School of Medicine, Baltimore, USA.

CRP: C-reactive protein is a plasma protein. The plasma concentration of CRP rise dur-
ing inﬂammation and infection of bacteria, parasites, or fungi [55]. CRP is therefore used
as a early marker of inﬂammation and infection [7].
HSV1 and CMV: Herpes Simplex Virus (HSV1) and Cytomegalovirus (CMV) are both viruses
of the herpesvirus family. Acute infection is followed by lifelong latent infection, and pe-
riodic reactivation of the latent infection is common [12]. Both infections are transmitted
from person-to-person contact, sexually, and non-sexually through various bodily secre-
tions [12].
Toxo: See section 2.1 regarding T. gondii.
3.5
Blood donor demographics
The 3973 blood donors included in this study were selected from the DBDS participants
by looking into data from the Danish National Patient Registry and evaluating their pre-
vious history of three classes of events: psychiatric disease (ICD-10: F00-F99), suicide
attempts, and trafﬁc accidents (ICD-10: V01-V99)[42]. The selection criteria of the donors
are presented in table 3.4. The study was designed as a case and control study. The case
groups where selected based on the presence of either of the above mentioned events.
The control group contain age and gender matched donors with no history of the men-
tioned events. This speciﬁc selection has been made due to the proposed association
between latent infections and neurological diseases, suicide rates, and increased rate of
trafﬁc accidents as described in section 2.1.5. Therefore this subset of donors have been
tested for the earlier mentioned latent infections, and C-reactive protein. The data is used
in other studies of the DBDS, to try verify these possible associations [42]. An overview of
the inclusion criteria are shown in table 3.4 and the blood donor demographics included
in the study are shown in table 3.5.

Chapter 4
Methods
4.1
Pipeline
The pipeline for obtaining the prediction models can be divided into 5 parts: acquir-
ing data, cleaning data, ﬁltering on missingness, preprocessing, and training classiﬁers.
A ﬂowchart of the pipeline is shown in ﬁgure 4.1. The data processing was done in
python3.6.1 through an anaconda3/4.4.0 installation. The packages: numpy, pandas,
sklearn, and keras was used. The code is available in appendix C.
4.2
Acquiring data
The data of the DBDS was contained in multiple separate ﬁles. All the data-ﬁles were
merged on the unique blood donation number (BDN) assigned to each blood donation.
At ﬁrst, the percentage of positive-infected donors in the dataset were rather low.
I
wanted to gain more data of postive-infected donors, as it would possibly enhance the
classiﬁer performance having more infected donors to learn from. Therefore, the BDN of
the infection-positive donors where associated with their personal identiﬁcation number
(PIN). By the PIN, blood donations of a later batch was gathered and included in the
dataset. As a T. gondii-infection is normally maintained for life (section 2.1.2), it was rea-
sonable to assume that the donors of the later batch are still infection-positive.
4.3
Cleaning data
The raw data received from the DBDS were cleaned by the steps outlined in the "clean-
ing data" box of ﬁgure 4.1. Measurements of more than ±3 median absolute deviations
(MAD) away from the median where considered to be outliers and therefore removed.
This approach was used as the mean is sensitive to outliers, and the method of using the
median and MAD has been proven to be more robust than using the mean and standard
deviation [44]. Next the haematology-features with missing values were replaced by an
estimate, obtained from their derived features, in the cases where the derived features
were not also missing (see section 3.2.2 and 3.2.3 for overview of derived features).
4.4
Filtering on missingness
The dataset contained many donors without T. gondii IgG-antibody measurements. Be-
fore ﬁltering away the donors without infection-status, the dataset including all donors
were saved to be used later for training of the missForest imputation algorithm. This
==PAGE:50==
32

• Convert to same units within features. 
• Remove units. 
• Remove physiologically unrealistic values and typos. 
• Remove outliers:  median ±3 median absolute deviation. 
• Calculate derived features and replace missing values where possible.

•

•

•

•

•

•

•

•

•
Train the classifiers on both training and validation-set using optimal hyper-parameters. 
•

FIGURE 4.1: Flowchart of the pipeline used to train the classiﬁers for pre-

==PAGE:51==
4.5. Preprocessing
33
dataset is referred to as the imputation-dataset (N=6224). The T. gondii-features were re-
moved from the imputation-dataset in order not to contribute with infection-information
when training the missForest. Next all donors without T. gondii-infection-status were
removed (2251 removed, hereby N=3973). As some of the features had a huge amount
of missing values, I decided to remove the features having more than 70% missing val-
ues. Furthermore, donors with more than 60% missing values where also removed (341
removed, N=3632). This was done as it was assumed to be hard for the imputation meth-
ods to give a reliable estimate of missing values in features and donors with this high
missingness.
Next, two version of the dataset where saved. One version referred to as the unﬁltered
dataset containing the full dataset (N=3632). The other one being ﬁltered as to having
no missing values in the CMV-features and RDW-SD, referred to as the ﬁltered dataset
(N=1175).
The prevalence of latent T-gondii-infections in the Danish population is approximately
23% [42], while in the unﬁltered and ﬁltered dataset the prevalence were 38.2% and
52.7%, respectively. The increased prevalence were obtained because of the ﬁltering and
as data from positive-infected donors of a later batch were added to the original dataset
(section 4.2).
4.5
Preprocessing
The blood-type-features were separated into two features. One feature containing the
rhesus-status and the other the AB0-group of the donor. Then all categorical features
were one-hot encoded (Health_status, Been_sad, AB0-group), as the classiﬁer-implementation
in sklearn does not support features encoded as strings.
Next a stratiﬁed 3-fold cross-validation loop of 0.15/0.85 was initialized. The split were
stratiﬁed according to infection-status, hereby insuring approximately the same distribu-
tion of positive and negative infected donors in all folds. The donors contained in the test-
set where removed from the imputation-dataset. This was done in order to avoid leakage
of information about the test-set onto the training-set when imputing the training-set.
Next the missForest algorithm were trained on the imputation-dataset, and hereafter
used to impute the test- and train-set. After imputation, the BMI of the donors were cal-
culated from weight and height, and the donors were assigned a BMI-weight-class. Both
BMI and the BMI-class were added as features to the train- and test-data. The BMI-class
was also one-hot encoded.
4.6
Training classiﬁers
In order to make an algorithm which can distinguish T. gondii-infected donors from non-
infected donors, various machine learning algorithms were trained. The algorithms I
chose to implement were a logistic regression, random forest and a neural network (NN).
As the infection-status of the donors are known, the classiﬁcation falls under the category:
supervised binary-classiﬁcation.
In order to select the hyper-parameters of each classiﬁer, a random-search over a classiﬁer-
speciﬁc hyper-parameter grid was run, using a stratiﬁed 5-fold cross-validation loop.
Random-search was chosen over grid-search as a study have found random-search to be
more efﬁcient [6]. Due to the time-limit of the project, random-search was not imple-
mented for the neural network, thus the hyper-parameters of the NNs were manually
==PAGE:52==
34
Chapter 4. Methods
tuned.
When training the logistic regression and NN, the training- and validation-data was nor-
malized. The normalization was ﬁtted only using the training-set in order not to leak
information about the validation-set to the training-set when scaling. Different scaling
methods was implemented in the hyper-parameter grid (section 2.3). In contrary to the
logistic regression and NN, random forest does not need data to be normalized before
training.
The ﬁnal classiﬁers were built by the hyper-parameters obtaining the best AUC perfor-
mance on the validation-set, and trained on both the training and validation-set. AUC
was chosen as performance score because it has been shown to be insensitive to class-
skewness (section 2.2.4, [24]). The ﬁnal models was then run on the test-set and the AUC,
precision, recall, f1-score and Matthews correlation coefﬁcient was calculated.
The imputation and classiﬁer training was repeated within each outer cross-validation
loop. Hereafter the mean performance-scores and standard deviation of the ﬁnals model
of the 3 runs were calculated. This was done in order to get an estimate of how robust
the classiﬁers were to different train/test splits and variability in imputation.
==PAGE:53==
35
Chapter 5
Results
The performance-scores for classifying T. gondii-infected donors obtained through this
project is presented for different subsets of the available dataset.
First, the data were sub-setted into a ﬁltered dataset, only containing donors without
missing values in the CMV-features and RDW-SD. This subsets is referred to as the ﬁl-
tered dataset, and the full dataset as the unﬁltered dataset. Secondly, both the unﬁltered
and ﬁltered datasets were further divided into datasets holding all available features and
subsets only holding the features which are regularly obtained at the Danish blood banks.
These features are referred to as the blood bank speciﬁc features and include the complete
blood count, age, gender, and the questionnaire-derived features.
The classiﬁers were trained on all subset of the dataset. This was done as to see which
performance could be achieved using all the features available for this project. And in
contrary, to see which performance could be expected if the models were to be imple-
mented at the blood banks, where only a subset of the features included in this study, are
regularly obtained.
All classiﬁers where trained in an outer 3-fold stratiﬁed cross-validation loop, hereby ob-
taining generalized performance-scores. In this section, mostly, the mean performance
scores obtained are presented. The results of each run are available in appendix A.
5.1
Unﬁltered dataset
The unﬁltered dataset held a total of 3632 donors and 43 features before one-hot encod-
ing. The prevalence of T. gondii- infected donors in the dataset were 38.2% (ﬁgure 5.10).

all available features whereas table 5.2 shows the performance-scores obtained using only
the blood bank speciﬁc features.

5.1.5
Missingness and class-dependencies of missing values
To get an overview of the missingness in the unﬁltered dataset, the fraction of missing
values in the train- and test-set is presented for each feature (ﬁgure 5.10). The fraction
above the bars indicate the missingness in the train and test-set respectively. The colored
bars indicate the fraction of T. gondii infected donors missing a value of the speciﬁc fea-
ture. It was found that all donors having a missing value in the CMV-feature were T.
gondii-infected. The missignness in the CMV-features were found to be 9%. The miss-
ingness in the HSV1- and CRP-features were about 0.43-0.47%. The fraction of postive-
and negative-infected donors having missing values in these feature were almost equal.
The RDW-SD and PCT features were found to have the highest amount of missingness of
around 58-61%. The missingness in the other haematology-derived features were around
10-26%. Almost no values were missing in the questionnaire-derived features.
==PAGE:62==
44
Chapter 5. Results
FIGURE 5.10: Missingness in train- and test-set in regards to T. Gondii-
infection status. Train-set represented in blue and test-set in green. Solid
colors represent the fraction of T. Gondii-positive donors and muted colors
T. Gondii-negative donors, having a missing value in the speciﬁc feature.
The fraction of missingness in the train- and test-set is displayed above
each bar.
Because the CMV-features was found to be most important for both random forest and lo-
gistic regression (ﬁgure 5.5 and 5.6), I decided to ﬁlter the dataset as to having no missing
values in the CMV-features. This was done to get an idea of how much the imputation
inﬂuenced the performance. The inﬂuence could potentially be high because the missing-
ness in CMV was about 9% and class-dependent (ﬁgure 5.10). Furthermore, the dataset
was also ﬁltered as to having no missing-values in the RDW-SD feature. This was done
as the missingness of the RDW-SD feature was high (58-60%) and early obtained results
indicated that RDW-SD was one of the most important haematology-derived features.
Moreover the missingness in RDW-SD was found to be slightly class-dependent (ﬁgure
5.10).

Filtered dataset
The ﬁltered dataset held a total of 1175 donors and 43 features before one-hot encoding.
The prevalence of T. gondii- infected donors in the dataset were 52.7% (ﬁgure 5.17). The
dataset was ﬁltered as to include only donors without any missing values in either RDW-
SD or the CMV-features.

respectively.
