53
Chapter 6
Discussion
Several studies have found latent T. gondii-infections in humans to be associated with
increased risk of psychiatric disorders, suicide attempts, and involvement in trafﬁc acci-
dents [16][58][49][43][25]. Moreover, acute T. gondii-infections have been shown to cause
serious complication if acquired by immune-compromised individuals. T. gondii is not
broadly accepted as a transfusion-transmissible infection, as only very few studies on the
subject have been made [68][2][19]. Therefore, no regularly screenings towards T. gondii
are obtained at the Danish blood banks. However, as few studies indicate, a potential
transmission risk could exist [68]. As of yet, the only available diagnostic tools for deter-
mining T.gondii-infections are all high-cost, low-throughput methods [45]. Therefore, if
T. gondii is truly transfusion-transmissible, a cheap and easy accessible tool for diagnosis
would be valued at the blood banks.
The aim of this study was to develop a low-cost, high-throughput method for predicting
T. gondii-infections, using machine learning. Such tool, could potentially reduce trans-
mission of T. gondii. Moreover it could be used to include T.gondii-infection-status in
association studies where only blood measurement are available.
6.1
Predicting latent T. gondii-infection in blood donors
In this study, a logistic regression, random forest, and neural network were trained on
data obtained from the Danish blood donor study. The classiﬁers were trained as to learn
a pattern of T. gondii-infection amongst the blood donors. By training the classiﬁers on
subsets of the DBDS dataset, I found that it was possible to predict T. gondii-infections
with a reasonable performance (table 5.4, 5.5, 5.1 and 5.2). The performance of the clas-
siﬁers were found to vary according to both ﬁltering of the data and the selection of
features included in the models.
The classiﬁer achieving the highest performance was a logistic regression model, reach-
ing a mean AUC on the test-sets of 0.859, MCC of 0.535, and precision, recall, f1-score,
and accuracy of approximately 0.77 (table 5.4). No previous studies have been made on
prediction of T. gondii-infections from regular blood measurements, why this is an inter-
esting and notable ﬁnding.
6.2
Feature importance for prediction
The features ranked to be of most importance in terms of predicting T. gondii-infection
were found to be CRP, CMV, HSV1 and age (ﬁgure 5.5, 5.6, 5.11 and 5.12).
Since the study were concerning latent infection, it makes good intuitive sense that age
were found to be a highly predictive factor. This is because, the longer one have lived,
the higher the probability of having been exposed to infection at some point in life.
However, the reason to why CMV and HSV1 were found to be important, are not so clear.
==PAGE:72==
54
Chapter 6. Discussion
It could be that the donors having multiple infection have been immune-compromised
at some point in their lives. During this period, they where more likely to obtain various
infections, including T. gondii, which could be the cause of this co-infection relation.
Moreover, HSV1 and CMV are sexually transmitted diseases, therefore the sexual risk
behaviour of the donors could maybe explain the ﬁndings. Studies have shown that T.
gondii is transmitted by semen in dogs, rabbits and sheep [1][46][47]. Even though no
studies have been made on sexually transmission of T. gondii in humans, unprotected sex
could be a possible route of transmission. Therefore, donors with risky sexual behaviour
might be more likely to acquire a T. gondii-infection, as well as HSV1 and CMV.
Furthermore, as T. gondii is known to transmit through poor food hygiene (section 2.1.2).
HSV1 and CMV could also indicate a personal trait of unhygienic behaviours, making it
more likely for donors with this trait to acquire a T.gondii-infection.
The importance of CRP for the learning algorithms might indicate that the CRP levels are
inﬂuenced by latent T. gondii-infection. This agree with literature which have found CRP
levels to increase during parasite infection [55]. However, CPR levels are more likely to
rise during the acute phase of infection [55].
When training the learning algorithms on the dataset only holding the blood bank spe-
ciﬁc features, age was found to be the most dominant feature for classiﬁcation (ﬁgure
5.13, 5.14, 5.8 and 5.9). Age was ranked many times higher than any other feature, in-
dicating that the other blood bank speciﬁc features might not hold much signal. When
training the classiﬁers on the same dataset, but without including age, it was also found
that the AUC dropped around 0.05-0.07 for the speciﬁc run (ﬁgure 5.15 and 5.16). How-
ever, the classiﬁers were still obtaining a decent performance, indicating that some signal
might be presented in the haematology-features.
Nonetheless, studies have shown that some haematology-measures are age-depended
[11][62] and that age can be predicted from biomarkers in blood by training neural net-
works [48]. Therefore, it could also be that when the classiﬁers are trained without includ-
ing age as a feature, a pattern of age is learned. Implying that the haematology-features
might not contribute with much information regarding infection-status but rather regard-
ing age. However, this is purely speculations and further studies are needed to clarity the
underlying relations.
Part from age, the most important features for prediction, when only using the blood
bank speciﬁc features were found to be MCHC, MCH, waist_measure, RDW-SD, RDW-
CV and given_birth (ﬁgure 5.13, 5.14, 5.8, 5.9, 5.15 and 5.16). The importance of the
given_birth feature were most dominant when removing age from the dataset (ﬁgure
5.15 and 5.16). This may indicate that given_birth is a predictor of age, as the older one
are, the more likely is it that one have given birth at some point - if you are a female of
course.
RDW-SD, RDW-CV are both measures of the variability in size of the red blood cells (sec-
tion 3.2.2). The T. gondii parasite has been shown to invade RBC in a in-vitro study [73].
Therefore, increase in variability in the size of the erythrocytes or abnormal erythrocytes
could be observed in T. gondii-infected donors, which could explain the feature impor-
tance of RDW-SD and RDW-CV. Furthermore, the parasite-erythrocyte-invasion theory
could also explain the importance of MCH and MCHC. MCH and MCHC are both mea-
sures of the amount of haemoglobin inside the red blood cells (section 3.2.2). However,
if the parasite invades the red blood cells, there could be less room for haemoglobin in
the red blood cells of infected donors. This would explain why MCH and MCHC were
ranked as important features. Yet, this is very speculative.
The importance of waist_measure and BMI could be explained by a study of the Danish
==PAGE:73==
6.3. Feature importance trustworthiness
55
blood donor study, showing that obesity is associated with risk of infection [39]. However
T.gondii-infection was not included in that study.
6.3
Feature importance trustworthiness
In relation to analysing the weight coefﬁcients and feature importance as ranked by the
classiﬁers, it should be noted that the importance-scores and the most used features, vary
to some degree at each run. Nevertheless, it was found that the logistic regression and
random forest classiﬁers used mostly the same top features for prediction, when applied
to the different data-subsets. Implying that information relating to T. gondii-infection, can
be extracted from the coefﬁcient weights and feature importance score, to some degree.
Further more, it should be noted that the coefﬁcient magnitudes of L1 regularized logistic
regressions can vary signiﬁcantly in cases where there are many correlated features in the
data. This is because sparse-solutions are sought when implementing L1-regularization.
L2 regularization could give a better picture of the features importance as it tends to ﬁnd
less sparse solutions and be more stable. However the main goal of the project was to
ﬁnd the most optimal prediction models, why both L1 and L2 penalization was included
as possible hyper-parameters for the logistic regression.
Nonetheless, all explanations of the ﬁndings in terms of features importance are very
speculative and further studies are needed to clarify the true underlying relations of T.
gondii and the features included in this study.
6.4
Performance differences between the ﬁltered and unﬁltered
dataset
A notable difference in the classiﬁer performance obtained when training on the ﬁltered
and unﬁltered dataset were found (table 5.6, 5.7 and 5.8). The classiﬁers trained on the
ﬁltered dataset obtained way the best performance scores. The difference in performance
on the two datasets have various plausible explanations.
First of all the missingness in the ﬁltered dataset is lower for all features, except the fea-
tures of HSV1 and CRP. This could indicate that the imputation is adding noise to the
signal. Hereby making it harder for the algorithms to learn the true pattern of infection.
Furthermore, it is likely that the ﬁltering simply removes many of the "hard-to-classify"
donors. This could happen due to randomness or because the missingness in the CMV-
features and RDW-CD is somehow related to the "hard-to-classify" donors.
6.5
Robustness to test/train-splits and imputation
The performance scores were found to vary very little between each fold in the outer
cross-validation loop. This indicate that the classiﬁers were robust to test- and training-
splits and to the non-deterministic behaviour of missForest.
6.6
Imputation-bias
From the missingness-plots (ﬁgure 5.10 and 5.17) it can be evaluated if the imputation
methods is biasing the datasets in a direction that could make it easier for the classiﬁers
==PAGE:74==
56
Chapter 6. Discussion
to learn a pattern of infection. In the unﬁltered dataset (ﬁgure 5.10), it was found that
the missingness in the CMV-features were very class-dependent. This implies that if the
missing values in each feature are all replace by values within the same narrow range, the
classiﬁers could learn this pattern of missingness. This pattern of imputation could make
it easier for the classiﬁers to predict the correct class. However, from the ﬁltered dataset,
without missing values in the CMV-features, CMV were still found to be one of the most
important features for classiﬁcation (ﬁgure 5.11,5.12). This indicates that the missForest
imputation method were not introducing bias to the dataset.
From the missingness-plots it was also found that the amount of missingness in CRP and
HSV1 were about 40-60% (ﬁgure 5.10 and 5.17). This is a high amount of missing val-
ues, thus the imputation-method had a huge impact on the content of the features. As
discussed, CRP and HSV1 are both features of high importance in terms of predicting in-
fection. Therefore, it could be feared that much of the signal of infection contained in the
features, solely exists due to imputation-bias. However, the fraction of infection-positive
and negative donors having missing values lie around 50%, therefore it is assumed that
the imputation method did not introduce much bias and that the results therefore are
reliable.
6.7
Latent T. gondii-infection associations studies
As described in section 2.1.5, studies have found associations between latent infection
with T. gondii and increased risk of trafﬁc accidents, suicide attempts and psychiatric ill-
ness. However, no results in this study indicate that these features contribute with much
signal in terms of predicting latent T.gondii-infections. This non-ﬁnding could have hap-
pened because the inclusion criteria of case-donors, does not take into account when the
donors where ﬁrst infected with T. gondii. It would therefore be interesting to see if a
signal would be obtained in a study where only donors who got a psychiatric diagnose
after being T. gondii-infected were included as having a psychiatric diagnose. However it
could also be that the associations are simply not that strong.
Furthermore, several studies have found Rh-positive subjects to be protected against
some of the neurological and cognitive deﬁcits associated with latent T.gondii-infection
[26][28][29]. Therefore rhesus-status could be a feature which the classiﬁers would ﬁnd
useful for classifying T. gondii-infected donors. However, again no results in this study
indicate that rhesus-status is a useful feature for prediction, as the feature importance for
Rhesus was ranked very low.
As earlier described, the pathway of T. gondii-infection encompass parasite invasion of
leukocytes in the host (section 2.1.2). Unfortunately, no features in the dataset hold in-
formation regrading the size of the leukocytes. However a signal of infection could exist
in a leukocyte size-measure. This would be interesting to examine in a future study. The
only feature included in this study which were contributing with information about the
leukocytes were WBC. The white blood cell count is expected to rise during infection, but
mostly during acute infections. This could be why WBC was not found to be an impor-
tant feature for predicting latent T. gondii-infected donors in this study.
==PAGE:75==
6.8. Implementation of model at the blood banks
57
6.8
Implementation of model at the blood banks
In terms of implementing the models at the blood banks not all features included in this
study can be used. The features, CMV, HSV1, CRP, trafﬁc accident, psychiatric diagno-
sis, and suicide attempt, are only obtained for this speciﬁc cohort of donors and thus not
obtained following the regular procedures at the blood banks. Therefore, if the classiﬁers
were to be implemented at the clinics, the models only trained on the blood bank speciﬁc
features can be used.
It was found that the performance obtained when only using the blood bank speciﬁc fea-
tures were between 0.05-0.17 lower for all metrics compare to using all features (ﬁgure
5.6, 5.7 and 5.8). The highest mean AUC achieved using only the blood clinic features
were 0.768, obtained by a logistic regression classiﬁer (table 5.5 and 5.6). This perfor-
mance is reasonable, but not highly precise, thus, if implemented at the blood clinics, the
classiﬁers should only be used to select donors who are likely to be infected. Hereafter a
more precise infection-test on the selected donors should be made.
Furthermore, the questionnaire-derived features were also speciﬁcally obtained for this
cohort of blood donors and are not regularly obtained at the blood banks. They were in-
cluded as blood bank speciﬁc features, as they were assumed to be obtainable by minor
changes of the procedures at the blood banks. Nonetheless, most of the questionnaire-
features did not provide much information in terms of classiﬁcation (ﬁgure 5.8, 5.9, 5.13
and 5.14). BMI, weight, and height were ranked highest of the questionnaire-derived
features. These measures are assumed to be easily obtained from the blood donors, thus
implementing the models at the clinics would be plausible. Moreover, CRP where found
to be one of the most important features for prediction. CRP is a very common measure-
ment in the medical sector and could be implemented as a standard measure at the blood
banks. The results indicate that this would improve classiﬁcation performance compared
to only using the blood bank speciﬁc features (ﬁgure 5.11 and 5.5).
Part from implementation at the blood banks the models can also be useful in other stud-
ies of the Danish blood donation study. DBDS contains considerable amounts of data
on blood donors where only a small subset of the donor have been tested for T. gondii.
Therefore the models can be used to predict the infection-status of donors without T.
gondii-infection measurements. This would add more information to the datasets and
could be used as an extra feature in association studies.
6.9
Future perspectives
I was expecting the neural network to out-perform the other classiﬁers, however this
was not obtained in this study. The reason for this might be that within the time-span
of the project, I did not manage to implement random-search over the hyper-parameters
of the neural network. The performance scores presented by the neural network in this
study are therefore obtained by manual tuning of the NNs. My future work will focus on
implementing random-search over the hyper-parameters of the NN, hereby, hopefully
obtaining even better results. Furthermore, I will look into implementing transfer learn-
ing, as it has been shown to improve performance in small dataset settings [63]. Transfer
learning is likely to increase performance in this speciﬁc setting as a lot of unlabelled
data are available from the DBDS. This data can be used to pre-train a NN, and hereafter
initialize a neural network with the pre-trained model.
Moreover, I will experiment with ensembling of the classiﬁers obtained in this study, as
ensembling multiple classiﬁers have shown to improve performance in some cases [67].
==PAGE:76==
58
Chapter 6. Discussion
Moreover, it would be interesting to ensemble the classiﬁers of each outer cross-validation
loop, an hereafter calculate the AUC. This could give different results than obtained from
the meaned AUCs of each model.
If the models are to be implemented in a clinical settings it is important to determine
whether false-negatives or false-positives are preferred.
In the blood bank setting, I
would argue that FN are worse than FP, because it can have more serious consequences
to transmit disease through infected blood, than discarding non-infected blood. Further-
more, as the classiﬁcation models are not extremely accurate, further testing of possibly
infected donor blood would be needed anyway, hereby insuring that infection-negative
blood would not be discarded. However, if the models are to be used for association
studies, this dilemma becomes less trivial.
==PAGE:77==
59
Chapter 7
Conclusion
Toxoplasma gondii is a protozoan parasite infecting all warm-blooded animals, including
humans. T. gondii-infection has been shown to cause behavioural changes in rodents,
which make them more likely to be preyed by cats - the parasite’s deﬁnite host. The
parasite manipulation theory suggest that the parasite changes the behaviour of its host
as to increase chances of transmission to its deﬁnitive host, where it can sexually re-
produce. The world prevalence of T. gondii in humans is approximately 30%. Recent
studies have found associations between latent T. gondii-infections in humans and per-
sonality disorders, increased risk of trafﬁc accidents, and psychiatric diseases, including
schizophrenia. Furthermore, acute T. gondii-infections can have severe consequence for
immune-compromised patients, where it can lead to inﬂammation of the brain and eyes.
Moreover, if women acquire T. gondii-infection during pregnancy, the parasite is likely
to cross the placenta and cause severe changes in the child, here among, hydrocephalus,
hearing and vision abnormalities, and seizures.
In many studies T. gondii is stated to transmit through both blood transfusion and or-
gan transplantation. However, only few studies on the subject have been made. Due
to the few studies, regularly screening towards T. gondii are not obtained at the Danish
blood banks, why a potential transmission risk, between donor and recipient, could exist.
In this study, a low-cost and high-throughput methods for predicting T. gondii-infections
in blood donors was sought. The method should ideally only use the measurements reg-
ularly obtained at the Danish blood banks. By training a logistic regression, random for-
est, and neural network on T. gondii-labelled data from a selected subset of blood donors
(N=3973) of the Danish blood donor study (DBDS), it was possible to predict T. gondii-
infections with a good accurateness. When including all features available for the study
an AUC of 0.859 and MCC of 0.535 was achieved. When training the classiﬁers on only
the features regularly obtained a the Danish blood banks an AUC of 0.768 and MCC of
0.391 was reached. The classiﬁer achieving best performance was the logistic regression,
however the performance of all classiﬁers were found to be very equal.
Of all included features, CRP, CMV, HSV1, and age were found to be of most impor-
tance in terms of classiﬁcation. This indication of co-infection relation could be caused
by donor-phenotypes of risky sexual behaviour or cleanliness, or due to a donor history
of immune-suppression. Furthermore, the importance of age could be explained by the
fact that the study concerned latent infection. Of the features regularly obtained at the
blood banks, age, RDW-SD, RDW-CD, MCHC, and MCH were found to contribute the
most to classiﬁcation. These ﬁndings could indicate that the parasite affect the dimen-
sions of erythrocytes in infected hosts, which might be explained by an in-vitro study
ﬁnding T. gondii to invade erythrocytes [73].
However, all explanations of the ﬁndings in terms of feature importance are very specu-
lative and further studies are needed to clarify the true underlying relations of T. gondii
==PAGE:78==
60
Chapter 7. Conclusion
and the features included in this study.
The fact, that the classiﬁers make use of features already obtained at the blood banks,
makes it convenient to implement the algorithms as a diagnostic tool at the clinics. The
results obtained in this study indicate that by including additional measurements, such
as CRP, even better classiﬁcation performance could be expected. If T. gondii becomes
broadly accepted as a transfusion-transmittable infection, implementing CRP as part of
the standard procedure at the clinics should therefore be considered. The classiﬁers are
not yet accurate enough to be completely reliable. It is therefore proposed that the classi-
ﬁers could be used as a tool for selecting donors who are likely to be infected, and here-
after conduct more acknowledge tests of the selected blood samples, such as an ELISA.
Part from being used as a diagnostic tool in the blood banks, the classiﬁers can also con-
tribute with additional information in studies of the DBDS. DBDS holds large portion of
blood donor data where only a small subsets have been tested for T. gondii-infection. It is
therefore evident to use the algorithms trained in this study, to classify T. gondii-infection
among the donors of the DBDS. These ﬁndings could potentially be used to conduct
larger association studies. If implemented in an analyses, cautions should be taken in
terms of the accurateness of the classiﬁers.
The results of this study are truly notable in terms of classiﬁcation performance and po-
tential use. However, even better performance is expected to be achieved by more ﬁne-
tuning of the neural networks, and ensembling of the classiﬁers. This is to be done in
future work.

==PAGE:98==
80
Appendix B. Additional ﬁndings
FIGURE B.4: Hyper-parameters and score for decision tree.
B.2
Other imputation methods
B.2.1
Distribution-based-random-median-imputation
Distribution-based-random-median-imputation is an imputation method where missing
values of a feature is replaced by randomly sampled values from an approximated distri-
bution of the feature. The feature distributions are approximated by assuming all features
to be normal-distributed, using the median and the standard-deviation of the feature as
the mean and standard-deviation, respectively. The median is used instead of the mean,
as the median is more robust to outliers [44]. It the features correlate with other features
of the dataset, this can be included when approximating the feature distributions.
One disadvantage of this method is that it can only handle continuous data. Thus another
imputation method such as most-frequent-value-imputation (described in section B.2.2)
needs to be applied to the categorical features.
B.2.2
Most-frequent-value-imputation
Most-frequent-value-imputation is a imputation method where missing values of a fea-
ture is replaced by the most frequently observed value of that feature.
This method can be used when working with categorical features, where distribution-
based methods do not apply.
B.2.3
Imputation performance difference
Imputing the unﬁltered dataset using both the distribution-based methods and missFor-
est was tried-out. It was found that the performance obtain when training on dataset of
the two imputation-methods were almost equal (ﬁgure B.1). MissForest was chosen as
the imputation-method to use through-out the project, as it have proven to achieve good
results on lab-value-imputation in other studies [76].
Logistic Regression
Random Forest
Neural Network
missForest
0.795 (0.011)
0.819 (0.013)
0.796 (0.016)

tion methods, missForest and the custom distribution-based imputation.

def matthews_correlation ( y_true ,
y_pred ) :
778
""" Matthews
c o r r e l a t i o n
metric .
779
780
781
I t
i s
only
computed
as
a
batch−wise
average ,
not
g l o b a l l y .
782
783
Computes
the
Matthews
c o r r e l a t i o n
c o e f f i c i e n t
measure
f o r
q u a l i t y
784
o f
binary
c l a s s i f i c a t i o n
problems .
785
"""
786
y_pred_pos = K. round (K. c l i p ( y_pred ,
0 ,
1) )
787
y_pred_neg = 1 −y_pred_pos
788
789
y_pos = K. round (K. c l i p ( y_true ,
0 ,
1) )
==PAGE:121==
C.2. Preprocessing and training classiﬁers
103
790
y_neg = 1 −y_pos
791
792
tp = K.sum( y_pos ∗y_pred_pos )
793
tn = K.sum( y_neg ∗y_pred_neg )
794
795
fp = K.sum( y_neg ∗y_pred_pos )
796
fn = K.sum( y_pos ∗y_pred_neg )
797
798
numerator = ( tp ∗tn −fp ∗fn )
799
denominator = K. sqrt ( ( tp + fp ) ∗( tp + fn ) ∗( tn + fp ) ∗( tn + fn ) )
800
801
return numerator / ( denominator + K. epsilon ( ) )
802
803
804

def
r e c a l l ( y_true ,
y_pred ) :
1230
""" R e c a l l
metric .
1231
1232
Only computes a
batch−wise
average
o f
r e c a l l .
1233
1234
Computes
the
r e c a l l ,
a
metric
f o r
multi−l a b e l
c l a s s i f i c a t i o n
o f
1235
how many r e l e v a n t
items
are
s e l e c t e d .
1236
"""
1237
true_positives = K.sum(K. round (K. c l i p ( y_true ∗y_pred ,
0 ,
1) ) )
1238
possible_positives = K.sum(K. round (K. c l i p ( y_true ,
0 ,
1) ) )
1239
r e c a l l = true_positives / ( possible_positives + K. epsilon ( ) )
1240
return
r e c a l l

def
precision ( y_true ,
y_pred ) :
1243
""" P r e c i s i o n
metric .
1244
1245
Only computes a
batch−wise
average
o f
p r e c i s i o n .
1246
1247
Computes
the
p r e c i s i o n ,
a
metric
f o r
multi−l a b e l
c l a s s i f i c a t i o n
o f
1248
how many
s e l e c t e d
items
are
r e l e v a n t .
1249
"""
1250
true_positives = K.sum(K. round (K. c l i p ( y_true ∗y_pred ,
0 ,
1) ) )
1251
predicted_positives = K.sum(K. round (K. c l i p ( y_pred ,
0 ,
1) ) )
1252
precision = true_positives / ( predicted_positives + K. epsilon ( ) )
1253
return
precision
1254
precision = precision ( y_true ,
y_pred )
1255
r e c a l l = r e c a l l ( y_true ,
y_pred )
1256

2∗(( precision ∗r e c a l l ) /( precision+ r e c a l l ) )

def matthews_correlation ( y_true ,
y_pred ) :
1260
""" Matthews
c o r r e l a t i o n
metric .
1261
1262
1263
I t
i s
only
computed
as
a
batch−wise
average ,
not
g l o b a l l y .
1264
1265
Computes
the
Matthews
c o r r e l a t i o n
c o e f f i c i e n t
measure
f o r
q u a l i t y
1266
o f
binary
c l a s s i f i c a t i o n
problems .
1267
"""
1268
y_pred_pos = K. round (K. c l i p ( y_pred ,
0 ,
1) )
1269
y_pred_neg = 1 −y_pred_pos
1270
1271
y_pos = K. round (K. c l i p ( y_true ,
0 ,
1) )
1272
y_neg = 1 −y_pos
1273
1274
tp = K.sum( y_pos ∗y_pred_pos )
1275
tn = K.sum( y_neg ∗y_pred_neg )
==PAGE:130==
112
Appendix C. Python code
1276
1277
fp = K.sum( y_neg ∗y_pred_pos )
1278
fn = K.sum( y_pos ∗y_pred_neg )
1279
1280
numerator = ( tp ∗tn −fp ∗fn )
1281
denominator = K. sqrt ( ( tp + fp ) ∗( tp + fn ) ∗( tn + fp ) ∗( tn + fn ) )
1282
1283
return numerator / ( denominator + K. epsilon ( ) )
